              <span class="content-header__institution">Joint Genome Institute, United States<span class="content-header__institution_separator" aria-hidden="true">;</span>
              </span>
            </li>

                    <section
    class="article-section article-section--first"
   id="abstract"
  data-behaviour="ArticleSection"
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h2 class="article-section__header_text">Abstract</h2>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">The columbine genus <i>Aquilegia</i> is a classic example of an adaptive radiation, involving a wide variety of pollinators and habitats. Here we present the genome assembly of <i>A. coerulea</i> ‘Goldsmith’, complemented by high-coverage sequencing data from 10 wild species covering the world-wide distribution. Our analyses reveal extensive allele sharing among species and demonstrate that introgression and selection played a role in the <i>Aquilegia</i> radiation. We also present the remarkable discovery that the evolutionary history of an entire chromosome differs from that of the rest of the genome – a phenomenon that we do not fully understand, but which highlights the need to consider chromosomes in an evolutionary context.</p>




      <span class="doi doi--article-section"><a href="https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.001" class="doi__link">https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.001</a></span>
  </div>

</section>


                
                    <section
    class="article-section "
   id="s1"
  data-behaviour="ArticleSection"
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h2 class="article-section__header_text">Introduction</h2>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Understanding adaptive radiation is a longstanding goal of evolutionary biology (<a href="#bib82">Schluter, 2000</a>). As a classic example of adaptive radiation, the <i>Aquilegia</i> genus has outstanding potential as a subject of such evolutionary studies (<a href="#bib29">Hodges et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#bib30">Hodges and Derieg, 2009</a>; <a href="#bib40">Kramer, 2009</a>). The genus is made up of about 70 species distributed across Asia, North America, and Europe (<a href="#bib66">Munz, 1946</a>) (<a href="#fig1">Figure 1</a>). Distributions of many <i>Aquilegia</i> species overlap or adjoin one another, sometimes forming notable hybrid zones (<a href="#bib18">Grant, 1952</a>; <a href="#bib27">Hodges and Arnold, 1994b</a>; <a href="#bib49">Li et al., 2014</a>). Additionally, species tend to be widely interfertile, especially within geographic regions (<a href="#bib95">Taylor, 1967</a>).</p>
    <div
        id="fig1"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="fig1"
        data-asset-viewer-uri="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg"
        data-asset-viewer-width="1500"
        data-asset-viewer-height="1167"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">
          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">
            <span class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text__prominent">Figure 1</span> with 1 supplement <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig1" class="asset-viewer-inline__header_link">see all</a>
          </div>
    
    
            <div class="asset-viewer-inline__figure_access">
              <a href="https://elifesciences.org/download/aHR0cHM6Ly9paWlmLmVsaWZlc2NpZW5jZXMub3JnL2xheDozNjQyNiUyRmVsaWZlLTM2NDI2LWZpZzEtdjIudGlmL2Z1bGwvZnVsbC8wL2RlZmF1bHQuanBn/elife-36426-fig1-v2.jpg?_hash=9ZeLIMaoQTBIjTBbHDp2tE%2F8wIAU0o3KjL758fIgUHg%3D" class="asset-viewer-inline__download_all_link" download="Download"><span class="visuallyhidden">Download asset</span></a>
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg" class="asset-viewer-inline__open_link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><span class="visuallyhidden">Open asset</span></a>
            </div>
    
      </div>
    
          <figure class="captioned-asset">
          
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg" class="captioned-asset__link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">
              <picture class="captioned-asset__picture">
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.webp 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.webp 1x"
                      type="image/webp"
                      >
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.jpg 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg 1x"
                      type="image/jpeg"
                      >
                  <img src="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig1-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg"
                       
                       alt=""
                       class="captioned-asset__image"
                  >
              </picture>
              </a>
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Distribution of <i>Aquilegia</i> species.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">There are ~70 species in the genus <i>Aquilegia</i>, broadly distributed across temperate regions of the Northern Hemisphere (grey). The 10 <i>Aquilegia</i> species sequenced here were chosen as representatives spanning this geographic distribution as well as the diversity in ecological habitat and pollinator-influenced floral morphology of the genus. <i>Semiaquilegia adoxoides</i>, generally thought to be the sister taxon to <i>Aquilegia</i> (<a href="#bib17">Fior et al., 2013</a>), was also sequenced. A representative photo of each species is shown and is linked to its approximate distribution.</p>
</div>
          
                  <span class="doi doi--asset"><a href="https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.002" class="doi__link">https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.002</a></span>
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">Phylogenetic studies have defined two concurrent, yet contrasting, adaptive radiations in <i>Aquilegia</i> (<a href="#bib3">Bastida et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib17">Fior et al., 2013</a>). From a common ancestor in Asia, one radiation occurred in North America via Northeastern Asian precursors, while a separate Eurasian radiation took place in central and western Asia and Europe. While adaptation to different habitats is thought to be a common force driving both radiations, shifts in primary pollinators also play a substantial role in North America (<a href="#bib104">Whittall and Hodges, 2007</a>; <a href="#bib3">Bastida et al., 2010</a>). Previous phylogenetic studies have frequently revealed polytomies (<a href="#bib27">Hodges and Arnold, 1994b</a>; <a href="#bib77">Ro et al., 1997</a>; <a href="#bib104">Whittall and Hodges, 2007</a>; <a href="#bib3">Bastida et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib17">Fior et al., 2013</a>), suggesting that many <i>Aquilegia</i> species are very closely related.</p>
<p class="paragraph">Genomic data are beginning to uncover the extent to which interspecific variant sharing reflects a lack of strictly bifurcating species relationships, particularly in the case of adaptive radiation. Discordance between gene and species trees has been widely observed (<a href="#bib70">Novikova et al., 2016</a> and references 15, 34–44 therein; <a href="#bib93">Svardal et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#bib54">Malinsky et al., 2017</a>), and while disagreement at the level of individual genes is expected under standard population genetics coalescent models (<a href="#bib94">Takahata, 1989</a>) (also known as ‘incomplete lineage sorting’ [<a href="#bib2">Avise and Robinson, 2008</a>]), there is increased evidence for systematic discrepancies that can only be explained by some form of gene flow (<a href="#bib20">Green et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib70">Novikova et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#bib93">Svardal et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#bib54">Malinsky et al., 2017</a>). The importance of admixture as a source of adaptive genetic variation has also become more evident (<a href="#bib42">Lamichhaney et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib55">Mallet et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#bib72">Pease et al., 2016</a>). Hence, rather than being a problem to overcome in phylogenetic analysis, non-bifurcating species relationships could actually describe evolutionary processes that are fundamental to understanding speciation itself. Here we generate an <i>Aquilegia</i> reference genome based on the horticultural cultivar <i>Aquilegia coerulea</i> ‘Goldsmith’ and perform resequencing and population genetic analysis of 10 additional individuals representing North American, Asian, and European species, focusing in particular on the relationship between species.</p>




  </div>

</section>


                
                    <section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2"
  data-behaviour="ArticleSection"
  data-initial-state="closed"
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h2 class="article-section__header_text">Results</h2>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-1"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Genome assembly and annotation</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">We sequenced an inbred horticultural cultivar (<i>A. coerulea</i> ‘Goldsmith’) using a whole genome shotgun sequencing strategy. A total of 4,773,210 Sanger sequencing reads from seven genomic libraries (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp1">Supplementary file 1</a>) were assembled to generate 2529 scaffolds with an N50 of 3.1 Mbp (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp2">Supplementary file 2</a>). With the aid of two genetic maps, we assembled these initial scaffolds into a 291.7 Mbp reference genome consisting of 7 chromosomes (282.6 Mbp) and an additional 1027 unplaced scaffolds (9.13 Mbp) (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp3">Supplementary file 3</a>). The completeness of the assembly was validated using 81,617 full length cDNAs from a variety of tissues and developmental stages (<a href="#bib41">Kramer and Hodges, 2010</a>), of which 98.69% mapped to the assembly. We also assessed assembly accuracy using Sanger sequencing of 23 full-length BAC clones. Of more than 3 million base pairs sequenced, only 1831 were found to be discrepant between BAC clones and the assembled reference (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp4">Supplementary file 4</a>). To annotate genes in the assembly, we used RNAseq data generated from a variety of tissues and <i>Aquilegia</i> species (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp5">Supplementary file 5</a>), EST data sets (<a href="#bib41">Kramer and Hodges, 2010</a>), and protein homology support, yielding 30,023 loci and 13,527 alternate transcripts. The <i>A. coerulea</i> 'Goldsmith' v3.1 genome release is available on Phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/</a>). For a detailed description of assembly and annotation, see Materials and methods.</p>




  </div>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-2"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Polymorphism and divergence</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">We deeply resequenced one individual from each of ten <i>Aquilegia</i> species (<a href="#fig1">Figure 1</a> and <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig1s1">Figure 1—figure supplement 1</a>). Sequences were aligned to the <i>A. coerulea</i> 'Goldsmith' v3.1 reference using bwa-mem (<a href="#bib46">Li and Durbin, 2009</a>; <a href="#bib48">Li, 2013</a>) and variants were called using GATK Haplotype Caller (<a href="#bib59">McKenna et al., 2010</a>). Genomic positions were conservatively filtered to identify the portion of the genome in which variants could be reliably called across all ten species (see Materials and methods for alignment, SNP calling, and genome filtration details). The resulting callable portion of the genome was heavily biased towards genes and included 57% of annotated coding regions (48% of gene models), but only 21% of the reference genome as a whole.</p>
<p class="paragraph">Using these callable sites, we calculated nucleotide diversity as the percentage of pairwise sequence differences in each individual. Assuming random mating, this metric reflects both individual sample heterozygosity and nucleotide diversity in the species as a whole. Of the ten individuals, most had a nucleotide diversity of 0.2–0.35% (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2a</a>), similar to previous estimates of nucleotide diversity in <i>Aquilegia</i> (<a href="#bib10">Cooper et al., 2010</a>), yet lower than that of a typical outcrossing species (<a href="#bib45">Leffler et al., 2012</a>). While likely partially attributable to enrichment for highly conserved genomic regions with our stringent filtration, this atypically low nucleotide diversity could also reflect inbreeding. Additionally, four individuals in our panel had extended stretches of near-homozygosity (defined as nucleotide diversity &lt;0.1%) consistent with recent inbreeding (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig2s1">Figure 2—figure supplement 1</a>). <i>Aquilegia</i> has no known self-incompatibility mechanism, and selfing does appear to be common. However, inbreeding in adult plants is generally low, suggesting substantial inbreeding depression (<a href="#bib65">Montalvo, 1994</a>; <a href="#bib24">Herlihy and Eckert, 2002</a>; <a href="#bib105">Yang and Hodges, 2010</a>).</p>
    <div
        id="fig2"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="fig2"
        data-asset-viewer-uri="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg"
        data-asset-viewer-width="1500"
        data-asset-viewer-height="1490"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">
          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">
            <span class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text__prominent">Figure 2</span> with 2 supplements <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig2" class="asset-viewer-inline__header_link">see all</a>
          </div>
    
    
            <div class="asset-viewer-inline__figure_access">
              <a href="https://elifesciences.org/download/aHR0cHM6Ly9paWlmLmVsaWZlc2NpZW5jZXMub3JnL2xheDozNjQyNiUyRmVsaWZlLTM2NDI2LWZpZzItdjIudGlmL2Z1bGwvZnVsbC8wL2RlZmF1bHQuanBn/elife-36426-fig2-v2.jpg?_hash=F9iuXkxXPnTPwpPlZpyowyC0HfMJeCXp7uHtiEVJveo%3D" class="asset-viewer-inline__download_all_link" download="Download"><span class="visuallyhidden">Download asset</span></a>
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg" class="asset-viewer-inline__open_link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><span class="visuallyhidden">Open asset</span></a>
            </div>
    
      </div>
    
          <figure class="captioned-asset">
          
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg" class="captioned-asset__link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">
              <picture class="captioned-asset__picture">
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.webp 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.webp 1x"
                      type="image/webp"
                      >
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.jpg 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg 1x"
                      type="image/jpeg"
                      >
                  <img src="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig2-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg"
                       
                       alt=""
                       class="captioned-asset__image"
                  >
              </picture>
              </a>
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Polymorphism and divergence in <i>Aquilegia</i>.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">(<b>a</b>) The percentage of pairwise differences within each species (estimated from individual heterozygosity) and between species (divergence). <math id="inf1"><mrow><msub><mtext>F</mtext><mrow><mtext>ST</mtext></mrow></msub></mrow></math> values between geographic regions are given on the lower half of the pairwise differences heatmap. Both heatmap axes are ordered according to the neighbor joining tree to the left. This tree was constructed from a concatenated data set of reliably-called genomic positions. (<b>b</b>) Polymorphism within each sample by chromosome. Per-chromosome values are indicated by the chromosome number.</p>
</div>
          
                  <span class="doi doi--asset"><a href="https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.004" class="doi__link">https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.004</a></span>
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">We next considered nucleotide diversity between individuals as a measure of species divergence. Species divergence within a geographic region (0.38–0.86%) was often only slightly higher than within-species diversity, implying extensive variant sharing, while divergence between species from different geographic regions was markedly higher (0.81–0.97%; <a href="#fig2">Figure 2a</a>). <math id="inf2"><mrow><msub><mtext>F</mtext><mrow><mtext>ST</mtext></mrow></msub></mrow></math> between geographic regions (0.245–0.271) was similar to that between outcrossing species of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genus (<a href="#bib70">Novikova et al., 2016</a>), yet lower than between most vervet species pairs (<a href="#bib93">Svardal et al., 2017</a>), and higher than between cichlid groups in Malawi (<a href="#bib52">Loh et al., 2013</a>) or human ethnic groups (<a href="#bib60">McVean et al., 2012</a>). The topology of trees constructed with concatenated genome data (neighbor joining (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2a</a>), RAxML (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig2s2">Figure 2—figure supplement 2a</a>)) were in broad agreement with previous <i>Aquilegia</i> phylogenies (<a href="#bib26">Hodges and Arnold, 1994a</a>; <a href="#bib76">Ro and McPheron, 1997</a>; <a href="#bib104">Whittall and Hodges, 2007</a>; <a href="#bib3">Bastida et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib17">Fior et al., 2013</a>), with one exception: while <i>A. oxysepala</i> is sister to all other <i>Aquilegia</i> species in our analysis, it had been placed within the large Eurasian clade with moderate to strong support in previous studies (<a href="#bib3">Bastida et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib17">Fior et al., 2013</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">Surprisingly, levels of polymorphism were generally strikingly higher on chromosome four (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2b</a>). Exceptions were apparently due to inbreeding, especially in the case of the <i>A. aurea</i> individual, which appears to be almost completely homozygous (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2a</a> and <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig2s1">Figure 2—figure supplement 1</a>). The increased polymorphism on chromosome four is only partly reflected in increased divergence to an outgroup species (<i>Semiaquilegia adoxoides</i>), suggesting that it represents deeper coalescence times rather than simply a higher mutation rate (mean ratio chromosome four/genome at fourfold degenerate sites: polymorphism = 2.258, divergence = 1.201, <a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp6">Supplementary file 6</a>).</p>




  </div>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-3"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Discordance between gene and species trees</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">To assess discordance between gene and species (genome) trees, we constructed a cloudogram of trees drawn from 100 kb windows across the genome (<a href="#fig3">Figure 3a</a>). Fewer than 1% of these window-based trees were topologically identical to the species tree. North American species were consistently separated from all others (96% of window trees) and European species were also clearly delineated (67% of window trees). However, three bifurcations delineating Asian species were much less common: the <i>A. japonica</i> and <i>A. sibirica</i> sister relationship (45% of window trees), <i>A. oxysepala</i> as sister to all other species (30% of window trees), and the split demarcating the Eurasian radiation (31% of window trees). These results demonstrate a marked discordance of gene and species trees throughout both <i>Aquilegia</i> radiations.</p>
    <div
        id="fig3"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"

        data-asset-viewer-height="742"
    >

              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Discordance between gene and species trees.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">(<b>a</b>) Cloudogram of neighbor joining (NJ) trees constructed in 100 kb windows across the genome. The topology of each window-based tree is co-plotted in grey and the whole genome NJ tree shown in <a href="#fig2">Figure 2a</a> is superimposed in black. Blue numbers indicate the percentage of window trees that contain each of the subtrees observed in the whole genome tree. (<b>b</b>) Genome NJ tree topology. Blue letters a-c on the tree denote subtrees a-c in panel (<b>d</b>). (<b>c</b>) Chromosome four NJ tree topology. Blue letters d and e on the tree denote subtrees d and e in panel (<b>d</b>). (<b>d</b>) Prevalence of each subtree that varied significantly by chromosome. Genomic (black bar) and per chromosome (chromosome number) values are given.</p>
</div>
          
                  <span class="doi doi--asset"><a href="https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.007" class="doi__link">https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.007</a></span>
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">The gene tree analysis also highlighted the unique evolutionary history of chromosome four. Of 217 unique subtrees observed in gene trees, nine varied significantly in frequency between chromosomes (chi-square test p-value &lt; 0.05 after Bonferroni correction; <a href="#fig3">Figure 3b–d</a> and <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig3s1">Figure 3—figure supplements 1</a> and <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig3s2">2</a>). Trees describing a sister species relationship between <i>A. pubescens</i> and <i>A. barnebyi</i> were more common on chromosome one, but chromosome four stood out with respect to eight other relationships, most of them related to <i>A. oxysepala</i> (<a href="#fig3">Figure 3d</a>). Although <i>A. oxysepala</i> was sister to all other species in our genome tree, the topology of the chromosome four tree was consistent with previously-published phylogenies in that it placed <i>A. oxysepala</i> within the Eurasian clade (<a href="#bib3">Bastida et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib17">Fior et al., 2013</a>) (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig2s2">Figure 2—figure supplement 2b,c</a>). Subtree prevalences were in accordance with this topological variation (<a href="#fig3">Figure 3b–d</a>). The subtree delineating all North American species was also less frequent on chromosome four, indicating that the history of the chromosome is discordant in both radiations. We detected no patterns in the prevalence of any chromosome-discordant subtree that would suggest structural variation or a large introgression (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig3s3">Figure 3—figure supplement 3</a>).</p>




  </div>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-4"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Polymorphism sharing across the genus</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">We next polarized variants against an outgroup species (<i>S. adoxoides</i>) to explore the prevalence and depth of polymorphism sharing. Private derived variants accounted for only 21–25% of polymorphic sites in North American species and 36–47% of variants in Eurasian species (<a href="#fig4">Figure 4a</a>). The depth of polymorphism sharing reflected the two geographically-distinct radiations. North American species shared 34–38% of their derived variants within North America, while variants in European and Asian species were commonly shared across two geographic regions (18–22% of polymorphisms, predominantly shared between Europe and Asia; <a href="#fig4">Figure 4b,c</a>; <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig4s1">Figure 4—figure supplement 1</a>). Strikingly, a large percentage of derived variants occurred in all three geographic regions (22–32% of polymorphisms, <a href="#fig4">Figure 4d</a>), demonstrating that polymorphism sharing in <i>Aquilegia</i> is extensive and deep.</p>

        id="fig4"

<p class="paragraph">In all species examined, the proportion of deeply shared variants was higher on chromosome four (<a href="#fig4">Figure 4d</a>), largely due to a reduction in private variants, although sharing at other depths was also reduced in some species. Variant sharing on chromosome four within Asia was higher in both <i>A. oxysepala</i> and <i>A. japonica</i> (<a href="#fig4">Figure 4b</a>), primarily reflecting higher variant sharing between these species (Figure 6a).</p>




  </div>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-5"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Evidence of gene flow</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Consider three species, H1, H2, and H3. If H1 and H2 are sister species relative to H3, then, in the absence of gene flow, H3 must be equally related to H1 and H2. The D statistic (<a href="#bib20">Green et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib16">Durand et al., 2011</a>) tests this hypothesis by comparing the number of derived variants shared between H3, and H1 and H2, respectively. A non-zero D statistic reflects an asymmetric pattern of allele sharing, implying gene flow between H3 and one of the two sister species, that is that speciation was not accompanied by complete reproductive isolation. If <i>Aquilegia</i> diversification occurred via a series of bifurcating species splits characterized by reproductive isolation, bifurcations in the species tree should represent combinations of sister and outgroup species with symmetric allele sharing patterns (D = 0). Given the high discordance of gene and species trees at the individual species level, we focused on testing a simplified tree topology based on the three groups whose bifurcation order seemed clear: (1) North American species, (2) European species, and (3) Asian species not including <i>A. oxysepala</i>. In all tests, <i>S. adoxoides</i> was used to determine the ancestral state of alleles.</p>
<p class="paragraph">We first tested each North American species as H3 against all combinations of European and Asian (without <i>A. oxysepala</i>) species as H1 and H2 (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5a–c</a>). As predicted, the North American split was closest to resembling speciation with strict reproductive isolation, with little asymmetry in allele sharing between North American and Asian species and low, but significant, asymmetry between North American and European species (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5b</a>). Next, we considered allele sharing between European and Asian (without <i>A. oxysepala</i>) species (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5d,e</a>). Here we found non-zero D statistics for all species combinations. Interestingly, the patterns of asymmetry between these two regions were reticulate: Asian species shared more variants with the European <i>A. vulgaris</i> while European species shared more derived alleles with the Asian <i>A. sibirica</i>. D statistics therefore demonstrate widespread asymmetry in variant sharing between <i>Aquilegia</i> species, suggesting that speciation processes throughout the genus were not characterized by strict reproductive isolation.</p>
    <div
        id="fig5"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="fig5"
        data-asset-viewer-uri="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:36426%2Felife-36426-fig5-v2.tif/full/,1500/0/default.jpg"
        data-asset-viewer-width="939"
        data-asset-viewer-height="1500"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">

              </picture>
              </a>
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">D statistics demonstrate gene flow during <i>Aquilegia</i> speciation.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">D statistics for tests with (<b>a–c</b>) all North American species, (<b>d</b>) both European species, (<b>e</b>) Asian species other than <i>A. oxysepala</i>, and (<b>f</b>) <i>A. oxysepala</i> as H3 species. All tests use <i>S. adoxoides</i> as the outgroup. D statistics outside the green shaded areas are significantly different from zero. In (<b>a–e</b>), each individual dot represents the D statistic for a test done with a unique species combination. In (<b>f</b>), D statistics are presented by chromosome (chromosome number) or by the genome-wide value (black bar). In all panels, E = European and A = Asian without <i>A. oxysepala</i>. In some cases, individual species names are given when the geographical region designation consists of a single species. Right hand panels are a graphical representation of the D statistic tests in the corresponding left hand panels. Trees are a simplified version of the genome tree topology (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2b</a>), in which the bold sub tree(s) represent the bifurcation considered in each set of tests. H3 species are noted in blue while the H1 and H2 species are specified in black. (<a href="#fig5sdata1">Figure 5—source data 1</a>).</p>
</div>
          
                  <span class="doi doi--asset"><a href="https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.013" class="doi__link">https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.013</a></span>
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
        <div class="additional-assets">
          
        
          <dl class="additional-assets__list">
              <dt class="additional-asset__text" id="fig5sdata1">
                    <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Figure 5—source data 1</h6>
                  
                    <p class="caption-text__heading">(D statistics).</p>
                  

              </dt>
              
              <dd class="additional-asset__access">
              
                  <span class="doi doi--asset"><a href="https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.014" class="doi__link">https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.014</a></span>
              
              
                  <div>
                    <a class="additional-asset__link additional-asset__link--download" href="https://elifesciences.org/download/aHR0cHM6Ly9jZG4uZWxpZmVzY2llbmNlcy5vcmcvYXJ0aWNsZXMvMzY0MjYvZWxpZmUtMzY0MjYtZmlnNS1kYXRhMS12Mi50eHQ-/elife-36426-fig5-data1-v2.txt?_hash=Ce%2Bxvhyu6qyT3X0AZxO8m9%2BaQTwKDlxl6%2BpC9vGLOaI%3D" download="elife-36426-fig5-data1-v2.txt">Download elife-36426-fig5-data1-v2.txt</a>
                  </div>
              </dd>
          </dl>
        
        </div>
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">Although non-zero D statistics are usually interpreted as being due to gene flow in the form of admixture between species, they can also result from gene flow between incipient species. Either way, speciation precedes reproductive isolation. The possibility that different levels of purifying selection in H1 or H2 explain the observed D statistics can probably be ruled out, since D statistics do not differ when calculated with only fourfold degenerate sites (<i>p</i>-value &lt; 2.2 x 10<sup>-16</sup>, adjusted R<sup>2 </sup>= 0.9942, <a href="#fig5sdata1">Figure 5—source data 1</a>). Non-zero D statistics could also indicate that the bifurcation order tested was incorrect, but even tests based on alternative tree topologies resulted in few D statistics that equal zero (<a href="#fig5sdata1">Figure 5—source data 1</a>). Therefore, the non-zero D statistics observed in <i>Aquilegia</i> most likely reflect a pattern of reticulate evolution throughout the genus.</p>
<p class="paragraph">Since variant sharing between <i>A. oxysepala</i> and <i>A. japonica</i> was higher on chromosome four (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6a</a>), and hybridization between these species has been reported (<a href="#bib49">Li et al., 2014</a>) we wondered whether gene flow could explain the discordant placement of <i>A. oxysepala</i> between chromosome four and genome trees (<a href="#fig3">Figure 3b,c</a>). Indeed, when the genome tree was taken as the bifurcation order, D statistics were elevated between these species (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5f</a>). A relatively simple coalescent model allowing for bidirectional gene flow between <i>A. oxysepala</i> and <i>A. japonica</i> (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6b</a>) demonstrated that doubling the population size (N) to reflect chromosome four’s polymorphism level (i.e. halving the coalescence rate) could indeed shift tree topology proportions (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6c</a>, row 2). However, recreating the observed allele sharing ratios on chromosome four (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6a</a>) required some combination of increased migration (m) and/or N (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6c</a>, rows 3–4). It is plausible that gene flow might differentially affect chromosome four, and we will return to this topic in the next section. Although the similarity of the D statistic across chromosomes (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5f</a>) might seem inconsistent with increased migration on chromosome four, the D statistic reaches a plateau in our simulations such that many different combinations of m and N produce similar D values (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6c</a> and <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig6s1">Figure 6—figure supplement 1</a>). Therefore, an increase in migration rate and deeper coalescence can explain the tree topology of chromosome four, a result that might explain inconsistencies in <i>A. oxysepala</i> placement in previous phylogenetic studies (<a href="#bib3">Bastida et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib17">Fior et al., 2013</a>).</p>

                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">The effect of differences in coalescence time and gene flow on tree topologies.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">(<b>a</b>) The observed proportion of informative derived variants supporting each possible Asian tree topology genome-wide and on chromosome four. Species considered include <i>A. oxysepala</i> (oxy), <i>A. japonica</i> (japon), and <i>A. sibirica</i> (sib). (<b>b</b>) The coalescent model with bidirectional gene flow in which <i>A. oxysepala</i> diverges first at time t2, but later hybridizes with <i>A. japonica</i> between t = 0 and t1 at a rate determined by per-generation migration rate, m. The population size (<b>N</b>) remains constant at all times. (<b>c</b>) The proportion of each tree topology and estimated D statistic for simulations using four combinations of m and Nvalues (t1 = 1 in units of N generations). The combination presented in the first row (m = 2x10<sup>-5 </sup>and N = 11667) generates tree topology proportions that match observed allele sharing proportions genomewide. Simulations with increased m and/or N (rows 3–4) result in proportions which more closely resemble those observed for chromosome four. Colors in proportion plots refer to tree topologies in (<b>a</b>), with black bars representing the residual probability of seeing no coalescence event. While this simulation assumes symmetric gene flow, similar results were seen for models incorporating both unidirectional and asymmetric gene flow (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig6s1">Figure 6—figure supplements 1</a> and <a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig6s2">2</a>).</p>
</div>
          
                  <span class="doi doi--asset"><a href="https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.015" class="doi__link">https://doi.org/10.7554/eLife.36426.015</a></span>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-6"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">The pattern of polymorphism on chromosome four</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">In most of the sequenced <i>Aquilegia</i> species, the level of polymorphism on chromosome four is twice as high as in the rest of the genome (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2b</a>). This unique pattern could be: (1) an artifact of biases in polymorphism detection between chromosomes, (2) the result of a higher neutral mutation rate on chromosome four, or (3) the result of deeper coalescence times on chromosome four (allowing more time for polymorphism to accumulate).</p>
<p class="paragraph">While it is impossible to completely rule out phenomena such as cryptic copy number variants (CNV), for the pattern to be entirely attributable to artefacts would require that half of the polymorphism on chromosome four be spurious. This scenario is extremely unlikely given the robustness of the result to a variety of CNV detection methods (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp7">Supplementary file 7</a>). Similarly, the pattern cannot wholly be explained by a higher neutral mutation rate. If this were the case, both divergence and polymorphism would be elevated to the same extent on chromosome four (<a href="#bib37">Kimura, 1983</a>). As noted above, this not the case (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp6">Supplementary file 6</a>). Thus the higher level of polymorphism on chromosome four must to some extent reflect differences in coalescence time, which can only be due to selection.</p>
<p class="paragraph">Although it is clear that selection can have a dramatic effect on the history of a single locus, the chromosome-wide pattern we observe (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig2s1">Figure 2—figure supplement 1</a>) is difficult to explain. Chromosome four recombines freely (<a href="#fig7">Figure 7a</a>), suggesting that polymorphism is not due to selection on a limited number of linked loci, such as might be observed if driven by an inversion or large supergene. Selection must thus be acting on a very large number of loci across the chromosome.</p>
    <div
        id="fig7"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "

        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"

<p class="paragraph">Balancing selection is known to elevate polymorphism, and in a number of plant species, disease resistance (<i>R</i>) genes show signatures of balancing selection (<a href="#bib35">Karasov et al., 2014</a>). While such signatures have not yet been demonstrated in <i>Aquilegia</i>, chromosome four is enriched for the defense gene GO category, which encompasses <i>R</i> genes (<a href="#table1">Table 1</a>). However, while significant, this enrichment involves a relatively small number of genes (less than 2% of genes on chromosome four) and is therefore unlikely to completely explain the polymorphism pattern (<a href="#bib69">Nordborg and Innan, 2003</a>).</p>

<p class="paragraph">Another potential explanation is reduced purifying selection. In fact, several characteristics of chromosome four suggest that it could experience less purifying selection than the rest of the genome. Gene density is markedly lower (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a> and <a href="#fig7">Figure 7b</a>), it harbors a higher proportion of repetitive sites (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>), and is enriched for many transposon families, including Copia and Gypsy elements (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#supp8">Supplementary file 8</a>). Additionally, a higher proportion of genes on chromosome four were either not expressed or expressed at a low level (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig7s2">Figure 7—figure supplement 2</a>). Gene models on the chromosome were also more likely to contain variants that could disrupt protein function (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>). Taken together, these observations suggest less purifying selection on chromosome four.</p>

        id="table2"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline--table "

        data-asset-viewer-group="table2"
    >

          </div>
    
    
            <div class="asset-viewer-inline__figure_access">
            </div>
    
      </div>

<p class="paragraph">Reduced purifying selection could also explain the putatively higher gene flow between <i>A. oxysepala</i> and <i>A. japonica</i> on chromosome four (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6</a>); the chromosome would be more permeable to gene flow if loci involved in the adaptive radiation were preferentially located on other chromosomes. Indeed, focusing on <i>A. oxysepala/A. japonica</i> gene flow, we found a negative relationship between introgression and gene density in the <i>Aquilegia</i> genome (<a href="#fig7">Figure 7c</a>, <i>p</i>-value = 2.202 x 10<sup>-7</sup>, adjusted R-squared = 0.068), as would be expected if purifying selection limited introgression. Notably, this relationship is the same for chromosome four and the rest of the genome (<i>p</i>-value = <math id="inf20"><mrow><mn>0.051</mn></mrow></math>), suggesting that gene flow on chromosome four is higher simply because the gene density is lower.</p>
<p class="paragraph">However, the picture is very different for nucleotide diversity. While there is a negative relationship between gene density and neutral nucleotide diversity genome-wide (<i>p</i>-value = 5.174 x 10<sup>-6</sup>, adjusted R<sup>2 </sup>= 0.052), more careful analysis reveals that chromosome four has a completely different distribution from the rest of the genome (<a href="#fig7">Figure 7d</a>, <i>p</i>-value &lt; 2 x 10<sup>-16</sup>). In both cases, there is a weak (statistically insignificant) negative relationship between gene density and nucleotide diversity (chromsome four: <i>p</i>-value = 0.0814, adjusted R<sup>2</sup> = 0.0303, rest of the genome: <i>p</i>-value = 0.315 , adjusted R<sup>2</sup> = 3.373 x 10<sup>-5</sup>), but nucleotide diversity is consistently much higher for chromosome four. Thus the genome-wide relationship reflects this systematic difference between chromosome four and the rest of the genome, and gene density differences alone are insufficient to explain higher polymorphism on chromosome four. Therefore, if reduced background selection explains higher polymorphism on this chromosome, something other than gene density must distinguish it from the rest of the genome. As noted above, there is reason to believe that purifying selection, in general, is lower on this chromosome.</p>
<p class="paragraph">For comparison with data from other organisms, we performed the partial correlation analysis of <a href="#bib11">Corbett-Detig et al. (2015)</a>. Here we found a significant relationship between neutral diversity and recombination rate (without chromosome four, Kendall’s tau = 0.222, <i>p</i>-value = 3.804 x 10<sup>-6</sup>), putting <i>Aquilegia</i> on the higher end of estimates of the strength of linked selection in herbaceous plants.</p>
<p class="paragraph">While selection during the <i>Aquilegia</i> radiation contributes to the pattern of polymorphism on chromosome four, the pattern itself predates the radiation. Divergence between <i>Aquilegia</i> and <i>Semiaquilegia</i> is higher on chromosome four (2.77% on chromosome four, 2.48% genome-wide, <a href="#table3">Table 3</a>), as is nucleotide diversity within <i>Semiaquilegia</i> (0.16% chromosome four, 0.08% genome-wide, <a href="#table3">Table 3</a>). This suggests that the variant evolutionary history of chromosome four began before the <i>Aquilegia</i>/<i>Semiaquilegia</i> split.</p>
    <div

    >

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-7"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">The 35S and 5S rDNA loci are uniquely localized to chromosome four</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">The observation that one <i>Aquilegia</i> chromosome is different from the others is not novel; previous cytological work described a single nucleolar chromosome that appeared to be highly heterochromatic (<a href="#bib51">Linnert, 1961</a>). Using fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH) with rDNA and chromosome four-specific bulked oligo probes (<a href="#bib23">Han et al., 2015</a>), we confirmed that both the 35S and 5S rDNA loci were localized uniquely to chromosome four in two <i>Aquilegia</i> species and <i>S. adoxoides</i> (<a href="#fig8">Figure 8</a>). The chromosome contained a single large 35S repeat cluster proximal to the centromeric region in all three species. Interestingly, the 35S locus in <i>A. formosa</i> was larger than that of the other two species and formed variable bubbles and fold-backs on extended pachytene chromosomes similar to structures previously observed in <i>Aquilegia</i> hybrids (<a href="#bib51">Linnert, 1961</a>) (<a href="#fig8">Figure 8</a>, last panels). The 5S rDNA locus was also proximal to the centromere on chromosome four, although slight differences in the number and position of the 5S repeats between species highlight the dynamic nature of this gene cluster. However, no chromosome appeared to be more heterochromatic than others in our analyses (<a href="#fig8">Figure 8</a>); FISH with 5-methylcytosine antibody showed no evidence for hypermethylation on chromosome four (<a href="/articles/36426/figures#fig8s1">Figure 8—figure supplement 1</a>) and GC content was similar for all chromosomes (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>). However, similarities in chromosome four organization across all three species reinforce the idea that the exceptionality of this chromosome predated the <i>Aquilegia</i>/<i>Semiaquilegia</i> split and raise the possibility that rDNA clusters could have played a role in the variant evolutionary history of chromosome four.</p>
    <div
